“Arra!” Run! Whoever it was grabbed her arm, pulled her forward, and pushed something heavy into her arms.

Smooth, heavy, unnaturally cool and slick… She ran her hands over it, already knowing what they would tell her. Angled sides, rounded corners, a flat top: it was the casket the Hett had been carrying. It was the Pilot Stone.

“Arra!” the voice repeated. She heard a wet cough, then a final wheezing rattle, and then she was alone. She hugged the casket to her chest, rose first to her knees and then to her feet.

The magical darkness cleared to reveal a scene of chaos. The bearded man’s body lay motionless beside her. She had slipped on his blood, she realized vaguely. It was on her clothes. She would have to wash them.

She looked up. By pure chance, she was hidden behind a pedestal whose statue had long since been stolen, smashed, or thrown off the mountain. She ducked her head and peered around the corner.

Razi was on his knees ten strides away, his hands behind his head, one crossbow against his back, another pointed at his chest from two strides away. The hilt of his sword lay just out of reach in a pool of quicksilver. Two bodies lay beside it.

Ten strides away from him, Erileine’s father and grandfather stood back to back surrounded by a circle of Hett. Somehow they had found each other in the darkness and not tried to run each other through by mistake.

With that thought, the world came back into focus. She looked at the casket in her arms. She had to get rid of it and its contents before she was discovered. The amphitheater ended in a cliff just twenty strides away. Heart in throat, she began to pick her way toward it.

“Drop your weapons now!” a familiar voice barked. “Do it, or I swear we will drop you.” Jaromir strode out of the tunnel mouth, fury on his face and a sword in his hand. Laeuc lowered his spear. A moment later, he elbowed his father-in-law hard in the ribs. Reluctantly, Vurt set his sword down at his feet, his face twisted in anger.

Jaromir halted in front of Razi. “Auedje ne atarappa paurenatepe ke fe iuadje te?” he demanded coldly.

Razi smiled politely. “La, my apologies, but I don’t speak—“

“Yes you do,” the Hett said in angry Gandan-o. “Where is the stone?”

Razi shrugged. “I swear, I do not know.”

“No?” Jaromir pointed his sword at Vurt. “And if I take this one’s head?”

“La, there is no need to murder,” Razi said, lowering his arms. The Hett behind the bookster jabbed his crossbow into Razi’s back, but the lean Karabandi ignored him.

“Murder?” Jaromir snarled. “You talk to me of murder? I see three good men dead here by your hand!”

“Hey, one of those was me,” Vurt said, sounding hurt.

Jaromir put the tip of his sword under Razi’s chin. “I am tired of my people dying, do you understand that? I am tired of people who have done no harm in this world being beaten by drunks, or driven from their homes by mobs, or robbed blind by petty lords who know that no one will hold them to account, not for the likes of us. Your mistress has done nothing to help us—nothing!—and neither has anyone else, so now we are going to help ourselves.”

“I grieve for the wrongs done to you and your people,” Razi said. “But please, this will not—“

He tilted his head back as Jaromir raised the tip of his sword. “Where. Is. The. Stone?”

Panic rose in Erileine’s throat. She fumbled with the latch on the casket, haste making her fingers clumsy. Snick. She lifted the lid.

Inside, a plain gray stone the size of her fist rested in a cast-iron spiderweb. She didn’t know what she had expected, but it wasn’t something as plain as this. But then she could suddenly feel the power coming out of the casket like a giant soundless heartbeat in time with the pulses of color from the machines by the pyramid. No—that was wrong. The machines were pulsing in time with the stone.

She looked back just as Jaromir’s patience snapped. “Very well. Sergeant!” A nearby soldier snapped a salute. Jaromir pointed at Vurt. “Make ready.”

Drawing his sword, the soldier stepped into place behind Erileine’s grandfather. When the old man turned to face him, the soldier grabbed his shoulder, spun him back around, and forced him to his knees.

“I will ask you again,” Jaromir said to Razi softly. “And then once more, and then it will be your turn.”

Razi looked up at him steadily. “All things in balance.”

Jaromir’s jaw tightened. He raised his arm. The Hett soldier brought his sword up.

“No!” Erileine shouted. Jaromir whirled around as she stood up at the edge of the cliff and raised the open casket. “Let them go or I’ll throw this over the side!”

“Neh!” Jaromir said, raising his hand to stop the half-dozen crossbowmen who had just aimed their weapons at her. If she fell backward, she and casket would both go over the edge.

“Let them go!” Erileine shouted. “Let them go or I’ll drop it, I swear on my blood I will!”

Jaromir laughed. “Go ahead. The Uremme Bech survived the Pilots’ war—it will survive a fall. And then it will call to us, and we will find it, and all you will have done is delay us by a few days.”

She hesitated. “Erileine…” her father pleaded. Vurt caught her eye and nodded, his back straight, his gaze unwavering.

Erileine sagged. As the soldier behind Vurt lowered his sword, she reached into the casket and yanked the stone out of the casket.

Or tried to. The moment her candlemarked hand touched the stone, something larger than the mountain—something as big as the whole world—noticed her, considered her briefly, and moved its attention elsewhere. She wanted to pull the covers over her head and hide from it. She wanted to beg it to come back and hold her close forever.

Lightning sparked up her arm. She collapsed on the floor, her muscles limp. As her head lolled to the side, the plain gray stone slipped out of her hand.

“Teppate me peua! Netahante adj auje!” Jaromir ordered. Rough hands picked Erileine up and carried her across the stone floor to lay her down beside Razi. The paralysis vanished as quickly as it had happened, its sudden absence leaving her shivering uncontrollably.

“Now watch,” Jaromir said. “Watch, so that your people will know what change has come.” Drawing a pair of gloves from his belt, he picked up the Pilot Stone and carried it to the pyramid. He bowed to the four magicians waiting there. Bowing in return, the gray-haired woman took it from him and mounted the stage.

The lights above the strange machines throbbed purple and blue, green and yellow, orange and deep, deep red. The magician joined her companions in a circle around the ram-horned statue that now stood on the pyramid’s flat top, a pair of bamboo struts bracing it so that it didn’t topple over.

Erileine could hear the machines now. They were singing to each other, great long notes that ran from bone-tingling bass to fluting whistles that pierced her ears. The air tingled as the light folded around itself and became a shining emptiness too bright to understand. The magicians chanted in time with the machines’ rhythmic pulse.

And then the Pilot Stone began to shine, except that wasn’t right. It wasn’t glowing: it was becoming more real. Erileine threw up her arm to cover her face, but the not-light went right through it—right through the whole world.

As one, the magicians on the stage cried out. Erileine lowered her arm just in time to see the light sucked into the statue’s mouth like smoke being inhaled. The statue opened its eyes and tilted its head to one side.

«Good.» The word appeared inside Erileine’s head like a sliver of ice dropped into a pool of water. Its eyes were steel marbles, Erileine noticed, dazed. Even without that, and the horns coiled on either side of its head where its ears should have been, and its hairlessness, and the complete lack of anything male or female between its legs, it was still too perfect to ever be mistaken for human.

“Aya! Aya! Aya!” the Hett shouted. “It is done! It is done! It is done!”

“My lord.” Tears ran down the Hett magician’s face, but her voice was strong. “My lord, welcome. Welcome back.”

«Who are these?» The statue’s thought took in Razi and the Avauntois.

“Unhearing ones, my lord. They tried to prevent your return.”

«They failed.»

“Yes, my lord.”

The statue pointed. «Return that one to the world.»

“No!” Erileine screamed as two Hett soldiers took Razi by the arms and pulled him to the cliff’s edge. “No! Please! Don’t!”

The bookster turned his head to look at her. “Be well, apprentice,” he called. With a sudden twisting shrug he pulled free from the soldiers holding him, and flung himself over the cliff.

“No!” Erileine screamed. “No, please…” She buried her face in her grandfather’s chest.

The statue swept its gaze around the chamber, taking in the now-silent machines and the reverent Hett. «This place is a mess.»

“Yes, my lord,” the magician replied.

«It needs mending.»

“Yes, my lord.” The magician’s voice was exultant.

«This world is broken.» Each word came perfectly formed into Erileine’s mind. «There are deserts where there should be gardens. The moon strays from its course.»

“Slow poison rots the heart of the world.” The Hett magician spoke the words aloud in time with the statue. Others joined in, some weeping with joy. “The ocean drains away and monsters roam free. It falls to us to mend it.”

«It falls to us to mend it.» the statue agreed. Its steel gaze fell on the Avauntois. «Go tell the world. The Great Work begins.»

One moment they were standing at the peak of the mountain. The next, they were on Slip Street outside the front door of their house.

The door flew open. “Erileine!” Saedanne rushed forward and pulled her daughter into a hug. “Oh, love, I was so worried.” Hot on her heels, Queçenne threw herself at her father.

Saedanne squeezed Erileine. “What happened? How did you get here?”

“I don’t know,” Erileine said, her voice muffled by her mother’s shoulder. “We were up at the peak, and—” She wasn’t going to cry.

“Wait.” Her mother looked past her at her husband. “Where’s Gaeston? Where’s the bookster?”

Laeuc shook his head wordlessly, his elder daughter still in his arms. Vurt put his hand on his son-in-law’s shoulder.

“They’re gone,” Erileine said dully. Her throat ached from the effort of not crying. Fraederiq, the captain, Uyves, Gaeston, Razi—even without the reanimated statue and whatever it was that had briefly touched her when she touched the stone, it was too much.

“Never mind that now,” Vurt said gruffly. “What the hells was that thing they woke up?”

“I think that was a Pilot,” Erileine said.

“A Pilot? But they’re all dead!” her grandfather protested.

Erileine shrugged. “Not any more.”

Anything she might have added was cut off as the ground lurched beneath them. Slowly but inexorably, the mountain began to tip over.

Here ends the first part of The Fall of the Flying Mountain.
